Refuting the Shia Interpretation of the Verse of Wilayah
The author refutes the Shia claim that the verse indicates Ali's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical Sunni refutation of the Shia claim that Quran 5:55 proves Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
The Verse of Wilayah does not prove Ali's imamate; 'wali' means ally, not ruler.

The Shia cite Quran 5:55 to argue that Ali is the wali and thus the imam after the Prophet. How do Sunni scholars respond? In this passage, the author presents several compelling arguments: the silence of the Companions, the lack of decisive proof, and the flexibility of the word 'wali'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shia claim
Start by reading the Shia argument presented at the beginning of the passage. They base their claim on the word 'wali' in the verse.
Notice how the author quotes the verse partially.
2
First refutation: historical argument
The author argues that if the verse meant Ali's imamate, the Companions would have known and Ali would have acted differently. Reflect on why this is a strong argument.
This argument uses consensus (ijma') and Ali's own actions.
3
Second refutation: lack of decisive proof
The author states that the verse is 'bila dalil' (without decisive proof). What does this mean in the context of kalam reasoning?
This shifts the burden of proof back to the Shia.
4
Third refutation: meaning of 'wali'
Even if the verse refers to Ali, 'wali' can mean ally or friend, not necessarily a ruler. Understand the linguistic nuance.
This is a key point about polysemy in Quranic interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول الروافض في آية (إِنَّمَا وَلِيُّكُمُ اللَّهُ ﴾ والرد عليه]: فإن قالوا أن الله تعالى قال: إِنَّمَا وَلِيُّكُمُ اللهُ وَرَسُولُهُ وَالَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا الَّذِينَ يُقِيمُونَ الصَّلوةَ () () قالوا: وهو على الله فوجب أن يكون وليا بعد الرسول عليه السلام، وإنما يكون وليًا أن لو كان متوليًا للإمامة فدل أنه الخليفة بعد الرسول عليه السلام. قلنا: لو كانت الآية منصرفة إلى ما قلتم لما خفي ذلك على الصحابة أولاً وعلى علي ثانيًا، ولما أجمعوا على خلافة غيره ولا بايع هو بنفسه غيره، ثم نقول: الآية بلا دليل، ولو جاز لهم حملها على علي له الله الجاز لغيرهم حملها على غيره، ولو سلم أن المراد به علي لم يلزم بإطلاق اسم الولي أن يكون إماما لأن الولي اسم يستعمل في الموالي، وذلك لا يستحيل في وصفه ولا يثبت به إمامته زمان أبي بكر رضي الله عنهما.
The statement of the Rafidah regarding the verse 'Your ally is only Allah...' (5:55) and the reply to it: For they say that Allah the Exalted said: 'Your ally is only Allah and His Messenger and those who believe, those who establish prayer...' They said: And he (Ali) is the wali of Allah, so it is necessary that he be the wali after the Messenger peace be upon him. And he is only a wali if he is in charge of the imamate, so it indicates that he is the caliph after the Messenger. We say: If the verse were directed to what you say, that would not have been hidden from the Companions first and from Ali second, and they would not have agreed upon the caliphate of another, nor would he himself have paid allegiance to another. Then we say: The verse is without definitive proof (dalil). And if it were permissible for them to interpret it as referring to Ali, it would be permissible for others to interpret it as referring to someone else. And even if we grant that the intended meaning is Ali, it does not necessarily follow from the mere use of the name 'wali' that he is an imam, because the word 'wali' is used for a patron or ally, and that is not impossible as a description of him, and it does not establish his imamate during the time of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with them both.
Source pages p. 1139

Commentary
This passage refutes the Rafidah's argument that Quran 5:55 proves Ali's imamate. The author first presents the Shia claim: Allah calls Ali a wali, so he must be the wali after the Prophet, meaning the imam. The author responds with several points. First, if the verse meant Ali's imamate, the Companions would not have agreed on Abu Bakr's caliphate, and Ali himself would not have pledged allegiance to others. Second, the verse is not explicit (it is without decisive proof). Third, even if it refers to Ali, the word 'wali' can mean ally or friend, not necessarily one who holds authority. Thus, it does not prove he was imam during Abu Bakr's time.

Key Terms
ولاية
ولي
إمامة
آية
روافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'wali' in the Quran always means a political ruler or successor.
Correction: In Quranic usage, 'wali' often means ally, friend, or protector, not necessarily a ruler. For example, Allah is called the Wali of believers.

Checkpoint
What is one reason the author gives against the Shia interpretation of the Verse of Wilayah?
A) The verse was abrogated.
B) Ali himself denied it.
C) If it meant Ali's imamate, the Companions would have known and acted differently.
D) The word 'wali' cannot be applied to Ali.

Key Takeaway
The Verse of Wilayah does not prove Ali's imamate because of historical consensus, lack of textual decisiveness, and the linguistic flexibility of 'wali'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Companions, including Ali, understood the Verse of Wilayah differently from what some claim today, what does that say about your own reliance on early Muslim consensus? Are you willing to submit to their understanding even when it challenges your preferences?
?
The word 'wali' can mean both 'friend' and 'ruler'. How would your relationship with Allah and His Prophet change if you focused more on being His ally than on seeking authority over others? What step can you take today to embody that meaning?
?
The author argues that if the verse were clear, Ali would have acted differently. Think of a belief you hold passionately. If it were truly from Allah, would your actions prove it? What would you change if you realized you had been prioritizing your own interpretation over the historical example of the righteous predecessors?

Refuting the Rafidah's Use of the Hadith of Wala
Refutation of the Rafidah's argument from the hadith of wala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Shia argument from the hadith 'O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him' by showing it is a supplication, not a claim of enmity against Abu Bakr, and by citing Ali's own testimony.

Core Idea
The hadith is a supplication, not a proof of succession or enmity.

The Rafidah (Shia) argue that the hadith 'O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him and be hostile to whoever is hostile to him' implies that Abu Bakr, being hostile to Ali, is an enemy of Allah. The author systematically refutes this by clarifying the hadith's nature and providing evidence of Ali's good relationship with Abu Bakr.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the Rafidah's claim
Start by reading the hadith and understanding the Rafidah's argument: that since Abu Bakr was supposedly hostile to Ali, the hadith means Allah is hostile to Abu Bakr.
The hadith is a supplication, not a declarative statement.
2
Understand the first refutation
The author first argues that the hadith is a supplication for Ali, not a proof of his succession. Even if Allah befriends Ali, that does not make him an imam.
Befriending is not the same as appointing to leadership.
3
Address the specific claim about Abu Bakr
The author denies that Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali or usurped his rights, pointing out that if he had, the Companions would not have supported him and Ali would not have pledged allegiance.
Historical consensus contradicts the claim of enmity.
4
Examine the evidence from Ali
The author cites reports from Ali himself affirming that Abu Bakr narrated to him and spoke the truth, showing their mutual respect and refuting the idea of enmity.
Ali's own testimony is the strongest refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا حجة لهم فيما يحتجون به من قولهم عليه السلام: 'اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه' لأن الحديث ليس فيه دليل استخلافه بل دعاء له وإظهار لفضيلته. يحققه إن كان من يواليه الله تعالى لا يكون إمامًا، فإن قالوا: إنه عليه السلام قال: عاد من عاداه وأبو بكر عادى عليا فيكون الله تعالى معاديًا له بقضية هذا الحديث. قلنا: إن أبا بكر لم يعاد عليا ولا غصب حقا له، ولو كان فعل ذلك لما اجتمعت معه الصحابة ولا بايعه علي له، فهذا من الروافض طعن على خيار الناس وصدور الأمة ونقله الدين باتباعهم من عاداه الله تعالى بدعاء رسوله، فطعن على علي وأتباعه من صار بمعاداته عدوا الله تعالى ثم قد ثبت عن علي أنه قال: حدثني أبو بكر، وصدق أبو بكر ، وروي عن علي الله عنه أنه قال : قال النبي عليه السلام لأبي
And there is no proof for them in what they argue with from his statement, peace be upon him: 'O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him and be hostile to whoever is hostile to him.' For the hadith contains no evidence of his succession but rather a supplication for him and a display of his virtue. This is confirmed by the fact that one whom Allah befriends is not necessarily an imam. For if they say: He, peace be upon him, said: 'Be hostile to whoever is hostile to him,' and Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali, then Allah would be hostile to him according to this hadith. We say: Abu Bakr was not hostile to Ali, nor did he usurp any right of his. Had he done that, the Companions would not have gathered with him, nor would Ali have pledged allegiance to him. This is the Rafidah's slander against the best of people and the leaders of the community and the transmitters of the religion, by claiming that they followed one whom Allah is hostile to due to His Messenger's supplication. Thus they slander Ali and his followers because by his enmity they become enemies of Allah. Then it has been established from Ali that he said: 'Abu Bakr narrated to me, and Abu Bakr spoke the truth.' And it is narrated from Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, said to Abu...'
Source pages p. 1140

Commentary
This passage refutes the Shia argument using the hadith 'O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him...'. The author first states that the hadith is a supplication for Ali, not a proof of his succession. Even if Allah befriends Ali, that does not make him imam. The Shia claim that since Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali, the hadith would mean Allah is hostile to Abu Bakr. The author replies that Abu Bakr was not hostile to Ali, nor did he wrong him. If he had, the Companions would not have supported him, and Ali would not have pledged allegiance. Moreover, the Shia argument insults the best of the community. Finally, the author provides explicit reports from Ali that Abu Bakr was truthful and that Ali narrated from him, showing their good relationship. The passage ends mid-sentence with a narration from Ali quoting the Prophet about Abu Bakr.

Key Terms
حديث
وال
عاد
أبو بكر
علي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him and be hostile to whoever is hostile to him' means that anyone who opposes Ali is an enemy of Allah.
Correction: The hadith is a supplication for Ali, not a declaration of enmity against those who differ with him. Moreover, Abu Bakr was not an enemy of Ali, as Ali himself testified.

Checkpoint
How does the author disprove the claim that Abu Bakr was an enemy of Ali?
A) By citing a verse from the Quran.
B) By arguing that Ali himself affirmed Abu Bakr's truthfulness and narrated from him.
C) By claiming the hadith is weak.
D) By saying the hadith was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The hadith does not prove enmity against Abu Bakr; Ali himself affirmed Abu Bakr's truthfulness, and the historical record shows no hostility between them.

Refutation of the Shia Interpretation of the Verse of Wilaya
Nasafi refutes the Shia claim that the verse designates Ali as caliph.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments used by Imam Nasafi to refute the Shia claim that a Qur'anic verse designates Ali as the immediate caliph.

Core Idea
The term 'wali' does not automatically mean political successor; context and consensus matter.

Imam Nasafi engages directly with a central Shia proof text. He offers a multi-layered refutation based on historical consensus, textual ambiguity, and semantic analysis. This lesson examines his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the Shia claim and Nasafi's first counter-argument
The Shia claim is that the verse points to Ali as caliph. Nasafi first argues that if it were clear, the Companions and Ali himself would not have missed it.
Historical consensus is a powerful evidence in Sunni thought.
3
Understand the second argument about textual ambiguity
Nasafi states the verse is without clear evidence, and if it could be interpreted for Ali, it could be interpreted for others.
This undermines the exclusivity of the interpretation.
4
Analyze the semantic point about the word 'wali'
Even if the verse referred to Ali, the word 'wali' does not necessitate imamah; it can mean friend or ally.
This is a crucial linguistic argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون وليًا أن لو كان متوليًا للإمامة فدل أنه الخليفة بعد الرسول عليه السلام. قلنا: لو كانت الآية منصرفة إلى ما قلتم لما خفي ذلك على الصحابة أولاً وعلى علي ثانيًا، ولما أجمعوا على خلافة غيره ولا بايع هو بنفسه غيره، ثم نقول: الآية بلا دليل، ولو جاز لهم حملها على علي لجاز لغيرهم حملها على غيره، ولو سلم أن المراد به علي لم يلزم بإطلاق اسم الولي أن يكون إماما لأن الولي اسم يستعمل في الموالي، وذلك لا يستحيل في وصفه ولا يثبت به إمامته زمان أبي بكر رضي الله عنهما.
He would be a wali only if he had been in charge of the imamate, so it indicates that he is the caliph after the Messenger (peace be upon him). We said: If the verse were referring to what you claim, it would not have been hidden from the Companions firstly nor from Ali secondly, and they would not have agreed upon the caliphate of someone else, nor would he himself have pledged allegiance to someone else. Then we say: The verse is without evidence, and if it were permissible for them to interpret it regarding Ali, it would be permissible for others to interpret it regarding someone else. And if it were granted that what is meant by it is Ali, the mere application of the name wali (friend/protector) does not necessitate that he be an imam, because the word wali is used in the sense of mawla (ally/friend), and that is not impossible in his description, nor does it establish his imamah during the time of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with them both.
Source pages p. 1140

Commentary
This passage refutes the Shia argument that a particular verse of the Qur'an (likely the verse of wilaya) designates Ali as the rightful caliph after the Prophet. The author makes several points: First, if the verse clearly meant Ali, it would not have been hidden from the Companions or from Ali himself; they would not have unanimously agreed on the caliphate of Abu Bakr, nor would Ali have pledged allegiance to him. Second, the verse itself is not explicit evidence for their claim. Third, even if the verse referred to Ali, the term 'wali' does not necessarily mean 'imam' or 'caliph'; it can mean friend or ally, and thus does not prove his political leadership during the time of Abu Bakr.

Key Terms
الولي
الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'wali' in the verse necessarily means political successor.
Correction: The word wali can mean friend or ally, and does not automatically imply leadership.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does the term 'wali' not prove Ali's imamah?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because 'wali' can mean friend or ally, not necessarily ruler.
C) Because Ali rejected the caliphate.
D) Because the verse was revealed after the event.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Shia interpretation by showing that the verse is ambiguous, the term wali is not synonymous with imam, and historical consensus contradicts their claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The word 'wali' can mean both friend and ruler. Are there any words or concepts in your faith that you conveniently interpret to suit your own desires? What safeguards have you built to prevent self-deception in understanding divine speech?

Refuting the Shia Interpretation of the Hadith 'Allahumma wali man walah'
Nasafi refutes the Shia claim that the hadith makes Ali the Imam and condemns Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith 'Allahumma wali man walah' does not support the Shia claim of Ali's immediate succession, and how Nasafi defends the integrity of Abu Bakr and the Companions.

Core Idea
The hadith is a supplication for Ali, not a designation of political leadership.

The hadith 'O God, befriend whoever befriends Ali and be hostile to whoever is hostile to him' is often used by the Shia to argue that Ali was the rightful Imam and that Abu Bakr was an enemy of God. In this passage, Nasafi dismantles this interpretation by clarifying the hadith's purpose, proving that Abu Bakr had no enmity towards Ali, and demonstrating that the Shia position leads to insulting the best of the Companions and even Ali himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the flow of argument: first the Shia claim, then Nasafi's counterpoints, then a conclusion.
The passage is dense. Take your time.
2
Identify Nasafi's three refutation points
The commentary highlights three main refutations: the hadith is a supplication, not a succession decree; the claim that Abu Bakr was hostile is false; and the Shia thereby insult the Companions and even Ali.
These correspond to logical steps in the argument.
3
Examine the key evidence from Ali himself
At the end, Nasafi quotes Ali saying that Abu Bakr narrated to him and spoke the truth. This shows that Ali had no enmity towards Abu Bakr.
This is a direct refutation of the Shia claim.
4
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider why this matter is important: it defends the integrity of the early Muslim community and the transmission of religious knowledge.
Also note the term 'Rawafid' used for the Shia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة كلامهم في حديث اللهم وال ثم بيان معنى الولي: ولا حجة لهم فيما يحتجون به من قولهم عليه السلام: 'اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه' لأن الحديث ليس فيه دليل استخلافه بل دعاء له وإظهار لفضيلته. يحققه إن كان من يواليه الله تعالى لا يكون إمامًا، فإن قالوا: إنه عليه السلام قال: عاد من عاداه وأبو بكر عادى عليا فيكون الله تعالى معاديًا له بقضية هذا الحديث. قلنا: إن أبا بكر لم يعاد عليا ولا غصب حقا له، ولو كان فعل ذلك لما اجتمعت معه الصحابة ولا بايعه علي له، فهذا من الروافض طعن على خيار الناس وصدور الأمة ونقله الدين باتباعهم من عاداه الله تعالى بدعاء رسوله، فطعن على علي وأتباعه من صار بمعاداته عدوا الله تعالى ثم قد ثبت عن علي أنه قال: حدثني أبو بكر وصدق أبو بكر.
Discussion of their statement concerning the hadith 'O God, befriend whoever befriends him and be hostile to whoever is hostile to him' then clarification of the meaning of wali: And there is no proof for them in what they argue with from his saying, peace be upon him: 'O God, befriend whoever befriends him and be hostile to whoever is hostile to him' because the hadith does not contain evidence of his succession, but rather it is a supplication for him and a manifestation of his virtue. He verifies that if one is a friend of God, that does not make him an imam. If they say: He, peace be upon him, said: 'Be hostile to whoever is hostile to him' and Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali, so God would be hostile to him according to the implication of this hadith. We said: Abu Bakr was not hostile to Ali nor did he usurp any right of his, and if he had done that, the Companions would not have gathered with him nor would Ali have pledged allegiance to him. This is from the Rawafid (extreme Shia) insulting the best of people and the leaders of the community and the transmitters of the religion by following those to whom God is hostile due to the supplication of His Messenger. Thus they insult Ali and his followers, for those who have hostility towards him become enemies of God. Then it is established from Ali that he said: Abu Bakr narrated to me and Abu Bakr spoke the truth.
Source pages p. 1140

Commentary
This passage refutes the Shia use of the famous hadith 'Allahumma wali man walah...' to claim that Ali was the rightful caliph and that Abu Bakr, because he allegedly opposed Ali, is an enemy of God. Nasafi makes several points: First, the hadith is a prayer and a declaration of Ali's virtue, not a designation of succession (istikhlaaf). Second, being a friend of God does not automatically make one an imam. Third, the claim that Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali is false; Abu Bakr did not wrong Ali. If he had, the Companions would not have accepted him, and Ali himself would not have pledged allegiance to him. Fourth, the Shia (Rawafid) thereby insult the best of the Companions. Fifth, it is authentically reported that Ali himself said that Abu Bakr narrated to him and that he (Ali) affirmed Abu Bakr's truthfulness, proving that there was no enmity between them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith 'Allahumma wali man walah' directly curses those who oppose Ali, including Abu Bakr.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the hadith is a supplication for Ali, not a curse on others, and Abu Bakr did not oppose Ali; rather, Ali testified to his truthfulness.

Checkpoint

B) By stating that Abu Bakr did not oppose Ali and that Ali himself testified to his truthfulness.
C) By saying that the hadith was abrogated.
D) By claiming that the hadith only applies to non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The hadith is a supplication for Ali, not a designation of succession, and Abu Bakr's relationship with Ali was one of mutual respect, not enmity.

Meanings of Mawla and Its Relevance to Caliphate
Author enumerates meanings of 'mawla' and rejects their connection to political authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple meanings of the word 'mawla' in Arabic and why none of them support the concept of caliphate or imamate.

Core Idea
'Mawla' means ally or friend, not political successor.

The term 'mawla' appears in a famous hadith about Ali, but its meaning is often misunderstood. In this lesson, we examine the linguistic possibilities presented by the author. By seeing the range of meanings, you'll appreciate why the author insists that the hadith cannot be about political leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the first part of the Arabic source where the author lists the meanings of 'mawla' with examples.
Notice the examples from hadith and poetry.
2
Identify the meanings
List the four meanings given: ally, loving friend, neighbor, son-in-law.
3
Note the author's conclusion
Read where the author says none of these meanings are built upon caliphate or imamate.
This is a key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر ويراد به الولي، والمحب الموالي، وقد روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: مزينة وجهينة وأسلم وعقار موالي الله ورسوله أي محبون موالون الله ورسوله، ويذكر ويراد به الجار. قال مربع بن دعدعة الكلابي وكان جاور كليب بن يربوع فأحمد جوارهم: جزى الله خيرا والجزاء يكفه كليب بن يربوع وزادهم حمدا هم حاطونا بالنفس وألجموا إلى نصر مولاهم مسومة جردا. ويذكر ويراد به الصهر قال المختار بن يزيد بن قيس الكلابي: فلا تنسين النافعين كليهما وهذا الذي في السوق مولى بني بدر. وكان الرجل صهرا لبني بدر. وشيء من هذه المعاني لا تبنى على الخلافة والإمامة ثم ما يليق بالحديث من هذه المعاني: "الناصر"، أي من كنت ناصره على دينه وحاميا له بباطني وظاهري فعلي ناصره وحاميه بباطنه وظاهره؛ فيكون دليلاً على طهارة سيرة علي وعلو رتبته إذ ليس كل ناصر بظاهره هذا رتبته في باطنه إذ قد يفعل ذلك طلبا للرياء والسمعة وابتغاء المال والفوز بالغنائم، ويحتمل أن يكون معناه من كنت محبوبا عنده فعلي محبوب عنده، وفائدة تخصيصه بذلك أنه عليه السلام ربما علم أن قوما ممن أضلهم الله تعالى وأعمى أبصارهم سيطعنون عليه، ويزعمون أنه خرج من الدين وفارقه، وحكم في أمر الله غير الله وسقطت بذلك ولايته وزال ولاؤه، فقال عليه السلام ذلك ليدل على بطلان قول
And it is mentioned and intended by it (mawla) the ally (al-wali), and the loving friend (al-muhibb al-muwali). And it has been narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'Muzaina, Juhaina, Aslam, and 'Aqar are the mawali of Allah and His Messenger,' meaning lovers and friends of Allah and His Messenger. And it is mentioned and intended by it the neighbor. Murabba' ibn Da'd'a al-Kilabi said—and he had neighbored Kulayb ibn Yarbu' and praised their neighborliness: 'May Allah reward goodly—and reward suffices Him—Kulayb ibn Yarbu', and increase them in praise. They protected us with their lives and urged towards the victory of their mawla, marked and swift horses.' And it is mentioned and intended by it the son-in-law. Al-Mukhtar ibn Yazid ibn Qays al-Kilabi said: 'Do not forget the two benefactors, both of them, and this one in the market is the mawla of the Banu Badr.' And the man was a son-in-law of Banu Badr. And none of these meanings are built upon the caliphate and imamate. Then what befits the hadith from these meanings is: 'the helper (al-nasir),' i.e., Whoever I am his helper in his religion and protector of him inwardly and outwardly, then Ali is his helper and his protector inwardly and outwardly. So it is evidence for the purity of Ali's conduct and the loftiness of his rank, since not every apparent helper has this rank inwardly, for he may do that seeking hypocrisy and reputation and seeking wealth and winning spoils. And it is possible that its meaning is: whoever I am beloved to him, then Ali is beloved to him. And the benefit of specifying him with that is that he ﷺ perhaps knew that some people, whom Allah has led astray and blinded their sight, would slander him, claiming that he left the religion and separated from it, and judged in Allah's affair other than Allah, and thereby his guardianship (wilaya) fell away and his loyalty (wala') ceased. So he ﷺ said that to indicate the falsehood of their statement.
Source pages p. 1141

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological analysis. The author first enumerates several meanings of the word 'mawla' (مولى) as used in Arabic: (1) the ally (الولي), (2) the loving friend (المحب الموالي) citing a hadith where tribes are called mawali of Allah and His Messenger meaning they love and befriend them, (3) the neighbor (الجار) with a poetic example, (4) the son-in-law (الصهر) with another poetic example. He then states that none of these meanings are built upon the concepts of caliphate and imamate. Next, he identifies the meaning that fits the well-known hadith 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla' as 'the helper' (الناصر). He unpacks this: the Prophet is the helper and protector of a person in his religion outwardly and inwardly, and so Ali shares that role. This demonstrates the purity of Ali's conduct and his high rank, because not every apparent helper has that inner sincerity; some help for show or gain. He also mentions an alternative interpretation: 'whoever I am beloved to him, then Ali is beloved to him.' Finally, he explains the benefit of singling out Ali in this hadith: the Prophet foresaw that some misguided people would later slander Ali, claiming he left the religion and judged by other than Allah's command, thus nullifying his guardianship (wilaya) and loyalty (wala'). So the Prophet said this to refute that false claim and prove that Ali's station remains intact.

Key Terms
مولى
الولي
الخليفة
الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' means Ali should be the caliph.
Correction: The author shows 'mawla' has many meanings unrelated to leadership, so the hadith does not establish political succession.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which meaning of 'mawla' is NOT mentioned in the passage?
A) The ally
B) The master
C) The neighbor
D) The son-in-law

Key Takeaway
The word 'mawla' has multiple linguistic meanings, but none of them inherently refer to political leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to let the language of the Arabs correct your interpretation, even if it challenges a deeply held narrative?
?
This passage shows that one word can have many meanings. Will you go back and re-evaluate that belief with the same linguistic rigor as this author?
?
The author concludes that none of these meanings support the caliphate. If you are a Sunni, does this passage strengthen your conviction—or does it expose a comfort zone where you rely on a text to justify a political position? If you are a Shia, does this passage provoke discomfort? Are you willing to sit with that discomfort and ask: 'What if I have been reading my own meaning into the text?'

Ali as Helper: The True Meaning of the Hadith
Author selects 'helper' as the intended meaning of 'mawla' for the hadith and explains its theological implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author chooses 'helper' as the meaning of 'mawla' in the hadith and how this interpretation affirms Ali's spiritual rank and refutes future slander.

Core Idea
Ali is the helper and protector of believers, just as the Prophet.

Having dismissed political meanings, the author now specifies that 'mawla' in the hadith means 'helper' (al-nasir). This lesson explores the author's reasoning, including the inward and outward dimensions of help, an alternative interpretation of love, and the prophetic foresight that the hadith would counter later attacks on Ali's character.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'then what befits the hadith' to the end.
Focus on how the author defines 'nasir' and its implications.
2
Understand the meaning 'helper'
The author says the Prophet is helper and protector of religion, and so is Ali. Note that this is both inward and outward.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
The author also suggests 'beloved' as a possible meaning, but highlights the benefit of singling out Ali.
4
Reflect on the benefit
The Prophet anticipated slander against Ali, so the hadith serves to refute false claims of Ali's deviation.
This shows the hadith is a defense of Ali's righteousness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر ويراد به الولي، والمحب الموالي، وقد روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: مزينة وجهينة وأسلم وعقار موالي الله ورسوله أي محبون موالون الله ورسوله، ويذكر ويراد به الجار. قال مربع بن دعدعة الكلابي وكان جاور كليب بن يربوع فأحمد جوارهم: جزى الله خيرا والجزاء يكفه كليب بن يربوع وزادهم حمدا هم حاطونا بالنفس وألجموا إلى نصر مولاهم مسومة جردا. ويذكر ويراد به الصهر قال المختار بن يزيد بن قيس الكلابي: فلا تنسين النافعين كليهما وهذا الذي في السوق مولى بني بدر. وكان الرجل صهرا لبني بدر. وشيء من هذه المعاني لا تبنى على الخلافة والإمامة ثم ما يليق بالحديث من هذه المعاني: "الناصر"، أي من كنت ناصره على دينه وحاميا له بباطني وظاهري فعلي ناصره وحاميه بباطنه وظاهره؛ فيكون دليلاً على طهارة سيرة علي وعلو رتبته إذ ليس كل ناصر بظاهره هذا رتبته في باطنه إذ قد يفعل ذلك طلبا للرياء والسمعة وابتغاء المال والفوز بالغنائم، ويحتمل أن يكون معناه من كنت محبوبا عنده فعلي محبوب عنده، وفائدة تخصيصه بذلك أنه عليه السلام ربما علم أن قوما ممن أضلهم الله تعالى وأعمى أبصارهم سيطعنون عليه، ويزعمون أنه خرج من الدين وفارقه، وحكم في أمر الله غير الله وسقطت بذلك ولايته وزال ولاؤه، فقال عليه السلام ذلك ليدل على بطلان قول
And it is mentioned and intended by it (mawla) the ally (al-wali), and the loving friend (al-muhibb al-muwali). And it has been narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'Muzaina, Juhaina, Aslam, and 'Aqar are the mawali of Allah and His Messenger,' meaning lovers and friends of Allah and His Messenger. And it is mentioned and intended by it the neighbor. Murabba' ibn Da'd'a al-Kilabi said—and he had neighbored Kulayb ibn Yarbu' and praised their neighborliness: 'May Allah reward goodly—and reward suffices Him—Kulayb ibn Yarbu', and increase them in praise. They protected us with their lives and urged towards the victory of their mawla, marked and swift horses.' And it is mentioned and intended by it the son-in-law. Al-Mukhtar ibn Yazid ibn Qays al-Kilabi said: 'Do not forget the two benefactors, both of them, and this one in the market is the mawla of the Banu Badr.' And the man was a son-in-law of Banu Badr. And none of these meanings are built upon the caliphate and imamate. Then what befits the hadith from these meanings is: 'the helper (al-nasir),' i.e., Whoever I am his helper in his religion and protector of him inwardly and outwardly, then Ali is his helper and his protector inwardly and outwardly. So it is evidence for the purity of Ali's conduct and the loftiness of his rank, since not every apparent helper has this rank inwardly, for he may do that seeking hypocrisy and reputation and seeking wealth and winning spoils. And it is possible that its meaning is: whoever I am beloved to him, then Ali is beloved to him. And the benefit of specifying him with that is that he ﷺ perhaps knew that some people, whom Allah has led astray and blinded their sight, would slander him, claiming that he left the religion and separated from it, and judged in Allah's affair other than Allah, and thereby his guardianship (wilaya) fell away and his loyalty (wala') ceased. So he ﷺ said that to indicate the falsehood of their statement.
Source pages p. 1141

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological analysis. The author first enumerates several meanings of the word 'mawla' (مولى) as used in Arabic: (1) the ally (الولي), (2) the loving friend (المحب الموالي) citing a hadith where tribes are called mawali of Allah and His Messenger meaning they love and befriend them, (3) the neighbor (الجار) with a poetic example, (4) the son-in-law (الصهر) with another poetic example. He then states that none of these meanings are built upon the concepts of caliphate and imamate. Next, he identifies the meaning that fits the well-known hadith 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla' as 'the helper' (الناصر). He unpacks this: the Prophet is the helper and protector of a person in his religion outwardly and inwardly, and so Ali shares that role. This demonstrates the purity of Ali's conduct and his high rank, because not every apparent helper has that inner sincerity; some help for show or gain. He also mentions an alternative interpretation: 'whoever I am beloved to him, then Ali is beloved to him.' Finally, he explains the benefit of singling out Ali in this hadith: the Prophet foresaw that some misguided people would later slander Ali, claiming he left the religion and judged by other than Allah's command, thus nullifying his guardianship (wilaya) and loyalty (wala'). So the Prophet said this to refute that false claim and prove that Ali's station remains intact.

Key Terms
مولى
الناصر
الولاية
الولاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' implies Ali's total authority, including political.
Correction: The author clarifies it means Ali is a helper and protector in religion, not a political ruler, and that the hadith is about spiritual support and love, not governance.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the benefit of the Prophet specifying Ali in the hadith?
A) To appoint Ali as his political successor
B) To increase Ali's wealth
C) To refute future slander against Ali
D) To make Ali the imam of prayer

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' reinforces Ali's role as a helper and protector of faith, not a political leader, and serves as a preemptive refutation of future accusations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet preemptively defended Ali against slander that had not yet occurred. Whom in your life might you be called to defend before they are even attacked? Will you take a stand, or remain silent out of fear?
?
This lesson calls you to be a true helper (nasir) in religion, inwardly and outwardly. Is your help to others pure? Or do you sometimes seek praise, reputation, or material gain? What is one hidden motive you need to repent from today?
?
If someone accused you of leaving the faith or betraying your principles, how would you want your closest friends to respond? Are you prepared to be that kind of defender for others, even when the accusation seems plausible?

Refuting the Objection on the Hadith's Wording
Author refutes the objection that the hadith should have been explicit about imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's method of refuting an objection by turning the argument around, and why demanding explicit wording from the Prophet is invalid.

Core Idea
Demanding explicit wording restricts the Prophet's wisdom.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that the hadith about 'mawla' should have been phrased more clearly to avoid ambiguity. The author counters by showing that if the hadith were indeed an explicit text for imamate, the Prophet would have said 'your imam after me,' but demanding such exact wording is an invalid restriction. The author then asserts that his own interpretation aligns with consensus and the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objectors propose that if the intended meaning was 'supporter', the Prophet should have said 'upon a believer in secret and public, pure of heart'.
Notice that they demand explicit wording.
2
See the author's reply
The author counters by saying that if the hadith were an explicit text for imamate, the Prophet would have said 'this is your imam after me'.
This turns the objection on its head.
3
Learn the principle of al-hajr 'ala al-rasul
The author declares that demanding such explicit wording is an invalid restriction on the Prophet's speech.
This is a methodological point about how we understand prophetic statements.
4
Consider the evidence
The author concludes that his interpretation is supported by ijma' and the Quran, while the opponents' interpretation is refuted by them.
This shows the author's reliance on primary sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو كان المراد ما ذكرتم لكان ينبغي أن يقول على مؤمن السر والعلانية نقي السريرة. وهو قلنا ولو كان المراد منه النص على الإمامة لقال النبي عليه السلام على إمامكم بعدي ثم هذا من باب الحجر على الرسول عليه السلام في الألفاظ فاسد، ثم ما حملنا الحديث عليه من المعنى لا يرده الإجماع ودلالة الكتاب، وما حملتم (3) الحديث عليه مردود بهما، والله الموفق.
For they say: If the intended [meaning] was what you mentioned, then he should have said 'upon a believer in secret and public, pure of heart.' And he is? We said: And if the intended [meaning] from it was the explicit designation of the imamate, the Prophet, peace be upon him, would have said 'upon your imam after me.' Then this is from the category of restricting the Messenger, peace be upon him, in expressions, which is invalid. Then what we have carried the hadith upon of meaning is not rejected by consensus (ijma') and the indication of the Book, and what you have carried (3) the hadith upon is rejected by them both. And Allah is the Success.
Source pages p. 1143

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objectors (likely the Rafidah) argue that if the intended meaning of 'mawla' in the hadith were 'supporter' as the author claims, then the Prophet should have explicitly said 'upon a believer in secret and public, pure of heart'—that is, he should have used clearer wording to avoid ambiguity. The author responds by turning the argument around: if the hadith were meant to be an explicit text (nass) for the imamate, the Prophet would have simply said 'this is your imam after me.' The author then states that it is improper to restrict the Prophet's choice of words (al-hajr 'ala al-rasul fi al-alfaz) because the Prophet spoke with wisdom and his words are not to be constrained by our expectations. Furthermore, the author's interpretation (that 'mawla' means supporter) is not contradicted by the consensus of the Companions or by the Quran; on the contrary, the Rafidah's interpretation is refuted by both. The passage ends with 'Wa Allah al-muwaffiq' (And Allah is the Grantor of success).

Key Terms

إجماع
كتاب
الحجر على الرسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about mawla should have been explicitly about imamate, and the Prophet's wording is insufficient.
Correction: The Prophet's wording is not to be restricted; his eloquence allows implicit meanings, and the author's interpretation is supported by consensus and the Quran.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the objection that the hadith should have been more explicit?
A) The Prophet did not need to be explicit because the meaning is clear enough.
B) If the hadith were an explicit text for imamate, the Prophet would have said 'this is your imam after me.', but such a demand is invalid restriction on the Prophet.
C) The hadith is explicit enough, and the objectors are simply ignorant.
D) The consensus of the Companions shows that the hadith is not about imamate.

Key Takeaway
The author defends his interpretation of the hadith by showing that demanding explicit wording is invalid and that his view is supported by consensus and the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself demanding that God or the Prophet speak to you in a specific, explicit way, rather than humbly accepting the wisdom of implicit guidance? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to impose conditions on revelation? Will you now commit to seeking deeper meaning without demanding surface-level clarity?
?
The author argues that restricting the Prophet's expressions is invalid. In your own life, are you restricting God's message by insisting on a certain form or wording? How can you surrender your expectations and trust in the eloquence of divine speech?
?
This lesson shows that the author's interpretation is validated by consensus and the Quran. How often do you measure your understanding of religion against the consensus of the early Muslims and the Quran? Are you willing to abandon your own biases for the sake of aligning with that standard?

Refuting the Argument from the Hadith of Position
Author refutes the claim that the 'Manzilat Harun' hadith proves 'Ali's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Hadith of Position (Manzilat Harun) does not prove 'Ali's caliphate, based on the consensus of the Companions and 'Ali's own actions.

Core Idea
The Companions' consensus and 'Ali's silence refute the Rafidah's use of the Hadith of Position.

The Rafidah often cite the famous hadith in which the Prophet tells 'Ali, 'You are to me as Aaron to Moses, except there is no prophet after me.' They claim this proves 'Ali's right to be caliph. Sanusi refutes this by pointing to the consensus of the Companions, who understood the hadith correctly, and to 'Ali's own failure to use it as proof when his right was contested.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Rafidah's claim that the Manzilat Harun hadith proves 'Ali's caliphate.
Note the hadith text: 'You are to me as Aaron to Moses, except no prophet after me.'
2
Understand the author's rebuke
Notice how the author sharply criticizes the Rafidah's lack of understanding before giving the substantive refutation.
This sets a polemical tone.
3
Analyze the consensus argument
The author argues that the Companions' unanimous support for Abu Bakr's caliphate proves the hadith does not imply political succession.
The Companions knew the hadith's meaning best.
4
Consider 'Ali's behavior
Notice that 'Ali himself did not use this hadith to claim leadership, even when his right was contested.
This is a strong pragmatic proof.
5
Conclude with the hadith's status
The author ends by stating the hadith is 'matruk' (abandoned) in terms of its apparent implication.
This completes the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا روى عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال لعلي ه: 'أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي (۳) وهذا دليل خلافته. قلنا: إنكم معشر الرافضة لقلة أفهامكم وبلادة أو هامكم تتعلقون بما لا حجة لكم فيه ولا دلالة فيه على ما ترون إثباته، ثم دلالة صحة ما ادعينا من انعدام دليل الإمامة في هذا الحديث إجماع الصحابة مع أنهم العارفون بأحاديث النبي عليه السلام ومعاني كلامه على خلافة أبي بكر ومساعدة على إياهم على ذلك، ولو كان فيما ذكرتم دلالة ما رمتم لما عدلوا عنه، ولا ترك علي له الاحتجاج به(٤) به وطلب ما هو أحق به من غيره على ما قررنا، وكذا في زمانه حتى نازعه غيره في الأمر الذي هو أولى به فاحتج بهذا الحديث، ولا تعلق به علمًا منه أنه لا دلالة له فيه. ثم الكشف عما ادعينا من عدم دلالة الحديث على ذلك أن هذا الحديث متروك
For they say: It was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said to 'Ali: 'Are you not pleased that you are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' (3) And this is evidence for his caliphate. We said: Indeed you, O group of Rafidah, due to the lack of your understanding and the dullness of your minds, cling to that which has no proof for you nor any indication of what you seek to establish. Then the proof of the correctness of what we claim of the absence of evidence for imamate in this hadith is the consensus of the Companions, while they are the knowers of the hadiths of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and the meanings of his speech, upon the caliphate of Abu Bakr and their helping him upon that. And if there were in what you mentioned an indication of what you desire, they would not have turned away from it, nor would 'Ali have refrained from using it as an argument (4) and seeking what he was more entitled to than others according to what we have established. And likewise in his time, until someone else contested him in the matter which he was more entitled to, he used this hadith as argument? And he did not cling to it, knowing that there is no indication in it. Then the disclosure of what we claim of the lack of indication of the hadith on that is that this hadith is abandoned (matruk).
Source pages p. 1143

Commentary
This passage addresses a second objection: the Rafidah cite the famous hadith in which the Prophet tells 'Ali, 'You are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me.' They claim this proves 'Ali's caliphate, since Aaron was Moses' successor. The author refutes this by first rebuking the Rafidah for their lack of understanding. He then provides a powerful argument from consensus (ijma'): the Companions, who best understood the Prophet's words, unanimously supported Abu Bakr's caliphate. If the hadith truly designated 'Ali, they would not have deviated from it, and 'Ali himself would have used it to claim his right. In fact, later when 'Ali's right was contested, he did not use this hadith as proof, because he knew it did not indicate imamate. Finally, the author states that the hadith is 'matruk' (abandoned) by the knowledge of the Companions, meaning its apparent implication is not acted upon. This completes the refutation.

Key Terms
منزلة هارون
إجماع الصحابة
حديث متروك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron to Moses' implies that 'Ali should have been the political successor of the Prophet.
Correction: The Companions understood the hadith correctly, as shown by their unanimous support for Abu Bakr, and 'Ali himself did not use it to claim leadership, proving it does not establish imamate.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument that the Hadith of Position does not prove 'Ali's caliphate?
A) The hadith is weak in transmission.
B) The Companions, including 'Ali, interpreted it as referring only to spiritual brotherhood.
C) The consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate, and 'Ali's own failure to cite this hadith in claim to power, prove it does not indicate imamate.
D) The Prophet explicitly said 'except there is no prophet after me' which limits the comparison to prophethood only.

Key Takeaway
The Hadith of Position does not prove 'Ali's caliphate, as shown by the Companions' consensus and 'Ali's own failure to use it as proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about religion that is not supported by the consensus of the righteous predecessors, what do you do with your pride? Will you cling to it, or humbly submit to the truth?
?
'Ali himself did not use this hadith to claim leadership. Are there any proofs you rely on that the very people they concern did not use? What does that say about your attachment to those proofs?
?
The Companions understood the Prophet's words better than anyone. When your understanding of a hadith contradicts their practice, is it your understanding or their practice that must yield? What will you change today?

Refuting Objections on the Prophet's Wording
Nasafi refutes a Rafidi objection about the wording of a hadith regarding imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Rafidi objection by arguing that demanding specific wording from the Prophet is an invalid restriction, and that the Sunni interpretation is upheld by consensus and the Quran.

Core Idea
Objecting to the Prophet's wording is an invalid restriction on him.

In this passage, Nasafi confronts a Rafidi objection: if the hadith truly indicated Abu Bakr's caliphate, its wording should have been different. Nasafi turns the argument around, showing that if the hadith were meant to designate 'Ali explicitly, the Prophet would have said 'your imam after me.' He then declares that the objection itself is an invalid restriction on the Prophet's freedom of expression, and concludes that the Sunni interpretation is supported by consensus and the Quran, whereas the Rafidi view is rejected.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Rafidi objection
Listen to the opponents' claim: they argue that the hadith's wording is not explicit enough for Abu Bakr's caliphate; if it were, the Prophet should have used a different phrase.
2
See Nasafi's counter-argument
Notice how Nasafi retorts: if the Prophet had intended to designate 'Ali as imam, he would have said 'on your imam after me' directly. The fact that he did not undermines the objection.
3
Recognize the invalid restriction
Nasafi argues that demanding a specific wording from the Prophet is an invalid restriction (hajar) on his speech. The Prophet is free to choose words that best convey his meaning.
4
Consider the evidence for Sunni interpretation
Note that Nasafi appeals to consensus (ijma') and the Quran as supports for the Sunni understanding, while the Rafidi interpretation is rejected by both sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو كان المراد ما ذكرتم لكان ينبغي أن يقول على مؤمن السر والعلانية نقي السريرة. قلنا: ولو كان المراد منه النص على الإمامة لقال النبي عليه السلام على إمامكم بعدي ثم هذا من باب الحجر على الرسول عليه السلام في الألفاظ فاسد، ثم ما حملنا الحديث عليه من المعنى لا يرده الإجماع ودلالة الكتاب، وما حملتم الحديث عليه مردود بهما، والله الموفق.
If they say: If the intended meaning was what you mentioned, then he should have said 'on a believer in secret and public, pure of conscience.' We say: And if the intended meaning was the explicit text for the imamate, then the Prophet peace be upon him would have said 'on your imam after me.' Then this is a matter of restricting the Messenger peace be upon him in expressions, which is invalid. Then what we have carried the hadith upon in terms of meaning, the consensus and the indication of the Book do not reject it, and what you have carried the hadith upon is rejected by both. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 1143

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents (Rafidah) object that if the Prophet's words were meant to indicate the caliphate of Abu Bakr as the author claims, the wording should have been different. The author replies by pointing out that if the Prophet had intended to explicitly designate Ali as imam, he would have said 'on your imam after me.' The author then argues that the objection itself is an invalid restriction on the Prophet's choice of words. He concludes that the Ahl al-Sunnah interpretation is supported by consensus (ijma') and the Quran, whereas the Rafidi interpretation is rejected by both.

Key Terms
الإمامة
النص
الإجماع
دلالة الكتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet should have used a specific wording to express his intention about the caliphate.
Correction: Demanding specific wording from the Prophet is an invalid restriction; the Prophet chose appropriate words.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the Rafidi objection about the wording of the hadith invalid?
A) Because the Prophet never used such phrases.
B) Because it would restrict the Prophet in his choice of words, which is invalid.
C) Because the hadith is not authentic.
D) Because the Rafidah reject the consensus of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Rafidi objection by arguing that it is an invalid restriction on the Prophet and that the Sunni interpretation is supported by consensus and the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the words of the Prophet ﷺ, do you ever find yourself wishing they were phrased differently to make things 'easier' or 'clearer' in your own mind? What does that reveal about your trust in his perfect eloquence? Commit this week to accepting his exact words without mental revision.
?
Are there any teachings of Islam that you struggle to accept because the primary sources don't match your personal expectations? How can you practice surrendering your own preferences to the words of the Prophet and the consensus of the scholars? Tonight, pick one such teaching and write down why you will submit to it.

The Rafidi Claim and Initial Refutation
Introduction of the Rafidi objection and Nasafi's initial critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Rafidi argument from the Hadith of Position and Nasafi's initial critique that the Rafidah misunderstand the hadith.

Core Idea
The Rafidah cling to a hadith that has no real proof for their claim.

The Rafidah (Shi'a) argue that the Prophet's statement 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' proves Ali's right to the caliphate. Nasafi begins his refutation by criticizing their understanding and then lays out a series of arguments. This lesson covers the objection and the first rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Rafidi objection
Read the first sentence where the Rafidah present their proof: the hadith of position. Notice how they interpret it as evidence for caliphate.
This is the claim Nasafi will refute.
2
Study Nasafi's immediate response
Nasafi responds by accusing the Rafidah of lack of understanding and dullness of minds. He says their proof has no evidence for what they seek to establish.
This is a rhetorical critique, but it sets the tone.
3
Identify the core of the argument
Nasafi does not yet provide his full refutation; he only asserts that the hadith lacks proof. This lesson focuses on the claim and the initial dismissal.
Later lessons will give detailed reasons.

فإن قالوا روى عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال لعلي: "أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي" وهذا دليل خلافته. قلنا: إنكم معشر الرافضة لقلة أفهامكم وبلادة أوهامكم تتعلقون بما لا حجة لكم فيه ولا دلالة فيه على ما ترون إثباته، ثم دلالة صحة ما ادعينا من انعدام دليل الإمامة في هذا الحديث إجماع الصحابة مع أنهم العارفون بأحاديث النبي عليه السلام ومعاني كلامه على خلافة أبي بكر ومساعدتهم على ذلك، ولو كان فيما ذكرتم دلالة ما رمت لما عدلوا عنه، ولا ترك علي له الاحتجاج به وطلب ما هو أحق به من غيره على ما قررنا، وكذا في زمانه حتى نازعه غيره في الأمر الذي هو أولى به فاحتج بهذا الحديث، ولا تعلق به علمًا منه أنه لا دلالة له فيه. ثم الكشف عما ادعينا من عدم دلالة الحديث على ذلك أن هذا الحديث متروك الظاهر بالإجماع فيما يصح تناوله إياه، وما هو المتنازع فيه لا دلالة له في الحديث عليه البتة، والتعلق بكلام لإثبات ما يتناوله ظاهره عند كونه متروك الظاهر ليس بصحيح فكيف التعلق به لإثبات ما يتناوله ظاهره ولا لفظه بوجه من الوجوه؟! وبيان ذلك أن هارون عليه السلام كان أخًا لموسى من أبيه وأمه، وكان شريكًا له في النبوة وتلقى الوحي من الله تعالى، ولم يكن هو خليفة لموسى عليه السلام بعد وفاته لأنه مات قبل موسى بسنتين، وأن عليًا رضي الله عنه لم تثبت له أخوة النبي عليه السلام لأبيه وأمه ولا شركته في النبوة والرسالة بقضية هذا الكلام، وإن كان ذلك ثابتا لهارون من موسى فكيف تثبت له الخلافة بعده ولم يكن ذلك ثابتا لهارون من موسى عليهما السلام فدل أن الاستدلال به في غاية البطلان. ثم نقول: لو عرفتم مخرج الحديث لما تعلقتم به وذلك لأن سبب الحديث أن النبي عليه السلام لما خرج إلى غزوة تبوك استخلف عليًا على المدينة، فأكثر أهل النفاق في ذلك، وزعموا أن النبي عليه السلام أبغضه وقالاه واستثقل صحبته، فاتبع علي رسول الله عليه السلام ولحق به وقال يا رسول الله أتتركني مع الأخلاف فقال: أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي أي: إني أستخلفتك بعد غيبتي عن المدينة عليها كما استخلف هارون عليه السلام حين غاب عن قومه لمناجاة ربه، وهذا يدل على رضاه باستخلافه على المدينة مدة غيبته عنها لا على أنه خليفته بعده كما في حق هارون عليه السلام، فإذا ليس فيه إثبات خلافته نصا ولا دلالة أيضا فإنه عليه السلام استخلف
If they say: It is narrated from the Prophet peace be upon him that he said to Ali: 'Are you not pleased that you are to me as Aaron was to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' And this is evidence of his caliphate. We say: Indeed, you, group of Rafidah, due to paucity of your understanding and dullness of your minds, cling to what has no proof for you nor any indication of what you seek to establish. Then the evidence of the correctness of what we claim — the absence of evidence for the imamate in this hadith — is the consensus of the Companions, despite their being the ones who know the hadiths of the Prophet and the meanings of his speech, upon the caliphate of Abu Bakr and their assistance in that. If there were in what you mention any indication [of their goal], they would not have turned away from it, nor would Ali have left off using it as proof and seeking what he was more entitled to than others, as we have established. Likewise in his time, until someone else disputed with him in the matter that was more fitting [for him], he did not use this hadith as proof, nor did he cling to it, knowing that there was no proof in it. Then the disclosure of what we claim — the lack of indication of the hadith on that — is that this hadith is abandoned in its apparent meaning by consensus regarding what can be applied to it, and what is disputed has no evidence in the hadith at all. To cling to speech to prove what its apparent meaning entails, when its apparent meaning is abandoned, is not valid. So how can one cling to it to prove what its apparent meaning entails, nor even its wording in any way?! The explanation of that is that Aaron peace be upon him was a brother of Moses from his father and mother, and was his partner in prophethood and received revelation from God Almighty, and he was not a successor (khalifa) of Moses after his death because he died two years before Moses. And Ali may God be pleased with him did not have established brotherhood with the Prophet peace be upon him from his father and mother, nor partnership in prophethood and messengership by virtue of this speech. And if that was established for Aaron from Moses, how can the caliphate after him be established for him, when that was not established for Aaron from Moses? It indicates that using it as evidence is extremely invalid. Then we say: If you knew the occasion of the hadith, you would not cling to it. That is because the occasion of the hadith is that when the Prophet peace be upon him left for the expedition of Tabuk, he appointed Ali as his deputy over Medina. Many of the hypocrites spoke about that and claimed that the Prophet hated him, was weary of him, and found his company burdensome. So Ali followed the Messenger of God and caught up with him and said: O Messenger of God, are you leaving me with the women and children? He said: 'Are you not pleased that you are to me as Aaron was to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' Meaning: I have appointed you as my deputy after my departure from Medina over it, just as Aaron appointed as deputy when he was absent from his people for the private conversation with his Lord. This indicates his pleasure with his deputyship over Medina during his absence, not that he was his successor after him, as was the case with Aaron. So there is no proof of his caliphate in it, explicitly nor by indication. Indeed, he appointed [Ali] as deputy
Source pages p. 1143

Commentary
This passage is a lengthy objection-and-answer. The Rafidah object that the Prophet's statement to Ali, 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' proves Ali's right to the caliphate. The author refutes this in several steps: First, he criticizes the Rafidah for clinging to a proof that has no real evidence. Second, he points to the consensus (ijma') of the Companions, who knew the hadiths best, in favor of Abu Bakr's caliphate. If the hadith had indicated Ali's caliphate, they would not have deviated from it. Third, the author notes that Ali himself did not use this hadith to claim the caliphate during disputes, indicating he knew it had no such proof. Fourth, the author debunks the analogy: Aaron was not Moses' successor (he died before Moses), so the comparison does not imply succession. Fifth, the author explains the occasion (sabab) of the hadith: it was spoken when the Prophet appointed Ali as his deputy in Medina during the Tabuk expedition, to reassure Ali against hypocrites' rumors. Thus, the hadith refers to temporary deputyship during absence, not to universal succession after death. The passage ends (cut off) with the author reiterating that the hadith only shows deputyship, not caliphate.

Key Terms
الخلافة
الإمامة
منزلة هارون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hadith of Position directly proves Ali's right to the caliphate.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the hadith has no such indication; it only shows deputyship during the Prophet's life.

Checkpoint
What do the Rafidah claim the Hadith of Position proves?
A) That Ali was a prophet
B) That Ali is the rightful caliph
C) That Ali was the best of the Companions
D) That Ali was the Prophet's brother

Key Takeaway
The Rafidah use the Hadith of Position to claim Ali's caliphate, but Nasafi dismisses it as lacking proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Islam that you later realized was based on a weak or misunderstood proof? How did you handle that realization, and what does this lesson teach you about seeking evidence even for cherished positions?
?
When you hear an argument that supports what you already believe, do you examine it critically or accept it readily? Could you be guilty of the 'paucity of understanding' Nasafi warns against? How will you guard against that from now on?

Refutation by Consensus and Ali's Silence
Nasafi argues from consensus of Companions and Ali's conduct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate and Ali's own silence disprove the Rafidi interpretation.

Core Idea
If the hadith proved Ali's caliphate, the Companions would not have chosen Abu Bakr, and Ali would have used it.

Nasafi continues his refutation by appealing to the historical fact that the Companions, who knew the hadith best, unanimously agreed on Abu Bakr's caliphate. Moreover, Ali himself never used this hadith to claim leadership. This lesson presents these two powerful arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus argument
Nasafi points to the ijma' of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate. If the hadith indicated Ali's right, they would not have deviated.
This is a strong argument from practice.
2
Examine Ali's behavior
Ali did not use this hadith to support his claim during disputes. Nasafi argues that if it were valid evidence, Ali would have used it.
This shows Ali himself did not interpret it as proof of caliphate.
3
Consider the implications
The combination of consensus and Ali's silence suggests the hadith has no bearing on caliphate.
These are external indicators, not an internal critique of the hadith.

According to Nasafi, why is the consensus of the Companions evidence against the Rafidi claim?
A) They were ignorant of the hadith

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When I hold firmly to a belief, do I ever check if the evidence I cite is truly as strong as I think—or could I be ignoring contrary signs like the silence of those closest to the source?
?
Imagine you were in Ali's position: you know a hadith that seemingly supports your right, yet you never use it. What would it take for you to remain silent? Is your faith in your own reasoning stronger than submission to collective wisdom?
?
Nasafi argues that the Companions' consensus proves the hadith's weakness. If the entire community of believers around me today consistently disagrees with my interpretation, am I humble enough to reconsider, or do I dismiss them as ignorant?

Refutation by Abandonment of Apparent Meaning and Aaron's True Status
Nasafi argues from abandonment of apparent meaning and the actual status of Aaron.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the hadith's apparent meaning is abandoned by consensus, and that the comparison with Aaron actually disproves succession.

Core Idea
The hadith's apparent meaning is rejected by consensus, and Aaron was not Moses' successor.

Nasafi now provides an internal critique of the hadith. He argues that the apparent meaning of the hadith (equating Ali to Aaron) is abandoned by consensus in matters it could apply to. Then he demonstrates that even if taken literally, Aaron was not Moses' successor, so the analogy cannot support Ali's caliphate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the abandonment argument
Nasafi says the hadith's apparent meaning is not followed by consensus in matters it could apply to. Therefore, using it to prove something beyond its wording is invalid.
This is a principle of hadith interpretation.
2
Analyze the Aaron comparison
Aaron was Moses' brother and partner in prophethood, but he died before Moses and was not his successor. So the comparison cannot imply succession for Ali.
This is a direct refutation of the Rafidi inference.
3
Consider the conclusion
Since the analogy fails, the hadith provides no evidence for caliphate. The Rafidi claim is therefore baseless.

If they say: It is narrated from the Prophet peace be upon him that he said to Ali: 'Are you not pleased that you are to me as Aaron was to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' And this is evidence of his caliphate. We say: Indeed, you, group of Rafidah, due to paucity of your understanding and dullness of your minds, cling to what has no proof for you nor any indication of what you seek to establish. Then the evidence of the correctness of what we claim — the absence of evidence for the imamate in this hadith — is the consensus of the Companions, despite their being the ones who know the hadiths of the Prophet and the meanings of his speech, upon the caliphate of Abu Bakr and their assistance in that. If there were in what you mention any indication [of their goal], they would not have turned away from it, nor would Ali have left off using it as proof and seeking what he was more entitled to than others, as we have established. Likewise in his time, until someone else disputed with him in the matter that was more fitting [for him], he did not use this hadith as proof, nor did he cling to it, knowing that there was no proof in it. Then the disclosure of what we claim — the lack of indication of the hadith on that — is that this hadith is abandoned in its apparent meaning by consensus regarding what can be applied to it, and what is disputed has no evidence in the hadith at all. To cling to speech to prove what its apparent meaning entails, when its apparent meaning is abandoned, is not valid. So how can one cling to it to prove what its apparent meaning entails, nor even its wording in any way?! The explanation of that is that Aaron peace be upon him was a brother of Moses from his father and mother, and was his partner in prophethood and received revelation from God Almighty, and he was not a successor (khalifa) of Moses after his death because he died two years before Moses. And Ali may God be pleased with him did not have established brotherhood with the Prophet peace be upon him from his father and mother, nor partnership in prophethood and messengership by virtue of this speech. And if that was established for Aaron from Moses, how can the caliphate after him be established for him, when that was not established for Aaron from Moses? It indicates that using it as evidence is extremely invalid. Then we say: If you knew the occasion of the hadith, you would not cling to it. That is because the occasion of the hadith is that when the Prophet peace be upon him left for the expedition of Tabuk, he appointed Ali as his deputy over Medina. Many of the hypocrites spoke about that and claimed that the Prophet hated him, was weary of him, and found his company burdensome. So Ali followed the Messenger of God and caught up with him and said: O Messenger of God, are you leaving me with the women and children? He said: 'Are you not pleased that you are to me as Aaron was to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' Meaning: I have appointed you as my deputy after my departure from Medina over it, just as Aaron appointed as deputy when he was absent from his people for the private conversation with his Lord. This indicates his pleasure with his deputyship over Medina during his absence, not that he was his successor after him, as was the case with Aaron. So there is no proof of his caliphate in it, explicitly nor by indication. Indeed, he appointed [Ali] as deputy
Source pages p. 1143

Commentary
This passage is a lengthy objection-and-answer. The Rafidah object that the Prophet's statement to Ali, 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' proves Ali's right to the caliphate. The author refutes this in several steps: First, he criticizes the Rafidah for clinging to a proof that has no real evidence. Second, he points to the consensus (ijma') of the Companions, who knew the hadiths best, in favor of Abu Bakr's caliphate. If the hadith had indicated Ali's caliphate, they would not have deviated from it. Third, the author notes that Ali himself did not use this hadith to claim the caliphate during disputes, indicating he knew it had no such proof. Fourth, the author debunks the analogy: Aaron was not Moses' successor (he died before Moses), so the comparison does not imply succession. Fifth, the author explains the occasion (sabab) of the hadith: it was spoken when the Prophet appointed Ali as his deputy in Medina during the Tabuk expedition, to reassure Ali against hypocrites' rumors. Thus, the hadith refers to temporary deputyship during absence, not to universal succession after death. The passage ends (cut off) with the author reiterating that the hadith only shows deputyship, not caliphate.

Key Terms
منزلة هارون
الإجماع
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' implies that Ali is the Prophet's successor just as Aaron was Moses' successor.
Correction: Aaron was not Moses' successor; he died before Moses. The hadith only indicates temporary deputyship during absence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the analogy with Aaron fails to prove Ali's caliphate?
A) Because Aaron was a prophet and Ali was not
B) Because Aaron died before Moses and was not his successor
C) Because Ali was not the Prophet's brother
D) Because the hadith is weak

Key Takeaway
The hadith's apparent meaning is abandoned by consensus, and Aaron's actual status disproves the Rafidi claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a literal meaning of a hadith when it contradicted the clear understanding of the majority of scholars? What might that reveal about your attachment to your own interpretation over communal authority?
?
The Rafidis used this hadith to support a political claim. In your own life, have you ever used a religious text selectively to justify a desire or a group loyalty? Would you be willing to abandon that interpretation if the evidence against it is clear?
?
If the Prophet did not intend for Ali to be his political successor by this hadith, how does that affect your view of the early caliphs? Are you ready to accept that the companions may have understood the Prophet's intentions better than later sectarian narratives?

The Occasion of the Hadith: Deputyship, Not Caliphate
Nasafi explains the occasion of the hadith and its true meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument from the occasion of the hadith (Tabuk expedition), showing that it refers to temporary deputyship in Medina, not universal succession.

Core Idea
The hadith was said to reassure Ali about his deputyship during Tabuk, not to indicate caliphate.

Nasafi finally explains the historical context of the hadith: it was spoken when the Prophet appointed Ali as his deputy in Medina during the Tabuk expedition, to comfort him against hypocrites' rumors. This context proves the hadith is about temporary deputyship during absence, not about succession after death.

Guided Path
1
Learn the occasion (sabab)
The Prophet appointed Ali as his deputy over Medina during the Tabuk campaign. Hypocrites spread rumors that the Prophet disliked Ali, so Ali followed him and asked if he was being left behind. The Prophet then said the hadith to reassure him.
This context is crucial.
2
Interpret the hadith in context
The Prophet meant: 'I have appointed you as my deputy over Medina while I am away, just as Aaron was deputy to Moses during his absence for the mountain.'
This is a temporary deputyship, not a permanent caliphate.
3
Contrast with the Rafidi claim
The Rafidah ignore the context and interpret it as a general statement of succession. Nasafi shows this is a misuse of the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا روى عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال لعلي: "أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي" وهذا دليل خلافته. قلنا: إنكم معشر الرافضة لقلة أفهامكم وبلادة أوهامكم تتعلقون بما لا حجة لكم فيه ولا دلالة فيه على ما ترون إثباته، ثم دلالة صحة ما ادعينا من انعدام دليل الإمامة في هذا الحديث إجماع الصحابة مع أنهم العارفون بأحاديث النبي عليه السلام ومعاني كلامه على خلافة أبي بكر ومساعدتهم على ذلك، ولو كان فيما ذكرتم دلالة ما رمت لما عدلوا عنه، ولا ترك علي له الاحتجاج به وطلب ما هو أحق به من غيره على ما قررنا، وكذا في زمانه حتى نازعه غيره في الأمر الذي هو أولى به فاحتج بهذا الحديث، ولا تعلق به علمًا منه أنه لا دلالة له فيه. ثم الكشف عما ادعينا من عدم دلالة الحديث على ذلك أن هذا الحديث متروك الظاهر بالإجماع فيما يصح تناوله إياه، وما هو المتنازع فيه لا دلالة له في الحديث عليه البتة، والتعلق بكلام لإثبات ما يتناوله ظاهره عند كونه متروك الظاهر ليس بصحيح فكيف التعلق به لإثبات ما يتناوله ظاهره ولا لفظه بوجه من الوجوه؟! وبيان ذلك أن هارون عليه السلام كان أخًا لموسى من أبيه وأمه، وكان شريكًا له في النبوة وتلقى الوحي من الله تعالى، ولم يكن هو خليفة لموسى عليه السلام بعد وفاته لأنه مات قبل موسى بسنتين، وأن عليًا رضي الله عنه لم تثبت له أخوة النبي عليه السلام لأبيه وأمه ولا شركته في النبوة والرسالة بقضية هذا الكلام، وإن كان ذلك ثابتا لهارون من موسى فكيف تثبت له الخلافة بعده ولم يكن ذلك ثابتا لهارون من موسى عليهما السلام فدل أن الاستدلال به في غاية البطلان. ثم نقول: لو عرفتم مخرج الحديث لما تعلقتم به وذلك لأن سبب الحديث أن النبي عليه السلام لما خرج إلى غزوة تبوك استخلف عليًا على المدينة، فأكثر أهل النفاق في ذلك، وزعموا أن النبي عليه السلام أبغضه وقالاه واستثقل صحبته، فاتبع علي رسول الله عليه السلام ولحق به وقال يا رسول الله أتتركني مع الأخلاف فقال: أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي أي: إني أستخلفتك بعد غيبتي عن المدينة عليها كما استخلف هارون عليه السلام حين غاب عن قومه لمناجاة ربه، وهذا يدل على رضاه باستخلافه على المدينة مدة غيبته عنها لا على أنه خليفته بعده كما في حق هارون عليه السلام، فإذا ليس فيه إثبات خلافته نصا ولا دلالة أيضا فإنه عليه السلام استخلف
If they say: It is narrated from the Prophet peace be upon him that he said to Ali: 'Are you not pleased that you are to me as Aaron was to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' Commentary
This passage is a lengthy objection-and-answer. The Rafidah object that the Prophet's statement to Ali, 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' proves Ali's right to the caliphate. The author refutes this in several steps: First, he criticizes the Rafidah for clinging to a proof that has no real evidence. Second, he points to the consensus (ijma') of the Companions, who knew the hadiths best, in favor of Abu Bakr's caliphate. If the hadith had indicated Ali's caliphate, they would not have deviated from it. Third, the author notes that Ali himself did not use this hadith to claim the caliphate during disputes, indicating he knew it had no such proof. Fourth, the author debunks the analogy: Aaron was not Moses' successor (he died before Moses), so the comparison does not imply succession. Fifth, the author explains the occasion (sabab) of the hadith: it was spoken when the Prophet appointed Ali as his deputy in Medina during the Tabuk expedition, to reassure Ali against hypocrites' rumors. Thus, the hadith refers to temporary deputyship during absence, not to universal succession after death. The passage ends (cut off) with the author reiterating that the hadith only shows deputyship, not caliphate.
